id="mw-head-base" class="noprint"></div>\n\t\t<div id="content" class="mw-body" role="main">\n\t\t\t<a id="top"></a>\n\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div id="siteNotice" class="mw-body-content"><!-- CentralNotice --></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t<div class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n</div>\n\t\t\t<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Latitude</h1>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div id="contentSub"></div>\n\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t\t<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">\n\t\t\t\t\tJump to:\t\t\t\t\t<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, \t\t\t\t\t<a href="#p-search">search</a>\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the geographical reference system. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Latitude_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Latitude (disambiguation)">Latitude (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Division_of_the_Earth_into_Gauss-Krueger_zones_-_Globe.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Division_of_the_Earth_into_Gauss-Krueger_zones_-_Globe.svg/200px-Division_of_the_Earth_into_Gauss-Krueger_zones_-_Globe.svg.png" width="200" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Division_of_the_Earth_into_Gauss-Krueger_zones_-_Globe.svg/300px-Division_of_the_Earth_into_Gauss-Krueger_zones_-_Globe.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Division_of_the_Earth_into_Gauss-Krueger_zones_-_Globe.svg/400px-Division_of_the_Earth_into_Gauss-Krueger_zones_-_Globe.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="948" data-file-height="948" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Division_of_the_Earth_into_Gauss-Krueger_zones_-_Globe.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nA graticule on the Earth as a <a href="/wiki/Sphere" title="Sphere">sphere</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Ellipsoid" title="Ellipsoid">ellipsoid</a>. The lines from pole to pole are lines of constant <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a>, or <b>meridians</b>. The circles parallel to the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a> are lines of constant latitude, or <b>parallels</b>. The graticule shows the latitude and longitude of points on the surface. In this example meridians are spaced at 6\xc2\xb0 intervals and parallels at 4\xc2\xb0 intervals.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Geography" title="Geography">geography</a>, <b>latitude</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Geographic_coordinate_system" title="Geographic coordinate system">geographic coordinate</a> that specifies the <a href="/wiki/North" title="North">north</a>\xe2\x80\x93<a href="/wiki/South" title="South">south</a> position of a point on the Earth\'s surface. Latitude is an angle (defined below) which ranges from 0\xc2\xb0 at the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">Equator</a> to 90\xc2\xb0 (North or South) at the poles. Lines of constant latitude, or <i>parallels</i>, run east\xe2\x80\x93west as circles parallel to the equator. Latitude is used together with <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">longitude</a> to specify the precise location of features on the surface of the Earth. Without qualification the term latitude should be taken to be the <i>geodetic latitude</i> as defined in the following sections. Also defined are six <i>auxiliary latitudes</i> which are used in special applications.</p>\n<p></p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc">\n<div class="toctitle">\n<h2>Contents</h2>\n</div>\n<ul>\n<li <span class="toctext">Inverse formulae and series</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Numerical_comparison_of_auxiliary_latitudes"><span class="tocnumber">4.8</span> <span latitude</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<p></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Preliminaries">Preliminaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latitude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Preliminaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Two levels of abstraction are employed in the definition of latitude and longitude. In the first step the physical surface is modeled by the <a href="/wiki/Geoid" title="Geoid">geoid</a>, a surface which approximates the <a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">mean sea level</a> over the oceans and its continuation under the land masses. The second step is to approximate the geoid by a mathematically simpler reference surface. The simplest choice for the reference surface is a <a href="/wiki/Sphere" title="Sphere">sphere</a>, but the geoid is more accurately modeled by an ellipsoid. The definitions of latitude and longitude on such reference surfaces are detailed in the following sections. Lines of constant latitude and longitude together constitute a <a href="/wiki/Geographic_coordinate_system" title="Geographic coordinate system">graticule</a> on the reference surface. The latitude of a point on the <i>actual</i> surface is that of the corresponding point on the reference surface, the correspondence being along the <a href="/wiki/Normal_(geometry)" title="Normal (geometry)">normal</a> to the reference surface which passes through the point on the physical surface. Latitude and longitude together with some specification of <a href="/wiki/Altitude" title="Altitude">height</a> constitute a <a href="/wiki/Geographic_coordinate_system" title="Geographic coordinate system">geographic coordinate system</a> as defined in the specification of the ISO 19111 standard.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[a]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Since there are many different <a href="/wiki/Reference_ellipsoid" title="Reference ellipsoid">reference ellipsoids</a>, the precise latitude of a feature on the surface is not unique: this is stressed in the ISO standard which states that "without the full specification of the coordinate reference system, coordinates (that is latitude and longitude) are ambiguous at best and meaningless at worst". This is of great importance in accurate applications, such as a <a href="/wiki/Global_Positioning_System" title="Global Positioning System">Global Positioning System</a> (GPS), but in common usage, where high accuracy is not required, the reference ellipsoid is not usually stated.</p>\n<p>In English texts the latitude angle, defined below, is usually denoted by the Greek lower-case letter <a href="/wiki/Phi_(letter)" class="mw-redirect" title="Phi (letter)">phi</a> (<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xcf\x86</span> or <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xcf\x95</span>). It is measured in <a href="/wiki/Degree_(angle)" title="Degree (angle)">degrees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arcminute" class="mw-redirect" title="Arcminute">minutes and seconds</a> or <a href="/wiki/Decimal_degrees" title="Decimal degrees">decimal degrees</a>, north or south of the equator.</p>\n<p>The precise measurement of latitude requires an understanding of the gravitational field of the Earth, either to set up <a href="/wiki/Theodolite" title="Theodolite">theodolites</a> or to determine GPS satellite orbits. The study of the <a href="/wiki/Figure_of_the_Earth" title="Figure of the Earth">figure of the Earth</a> together with its gravitational field is the science of <a href="/wiki/Geodesy" title="Geodesy">geodesy</a>.</p>\n<p>This article relates to coordinate systems for the Earth: it may be extended to cover the Moon, planets and other celestial objects by a simple change of nomenclature.</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Latitude_on_the_sphere">Latitude on the sphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latitude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Latitude on the sphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Latitude_and_longitude_graticule_on_a_sphere.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Latitude_and_longitude_graticule_on_a_sphere.svg/200px-Latitude_and_longitude_graticule_on_a_sphere.svg.png" width="200" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Latitude_and_longitude_graticule_on_a_sphere.svg/300px-Latitude_and_longitude_graticule_on_a_sphere.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Latitude_and_longitude_graticule_on_a_sphere.svg/400px-Latitude_and_longitude_graticule_on_a_sphere.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="825" data-file-height="825" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Latitude_and_longitude_graticule_on_a_sphere.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nA perspective view of the Earth showing how latitude (\xcf\x86) and longitude (\xce\xbb) are defined on a spherical model. The graticule spacing is 10 degrees.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_graticule_on_the_sphere">The graticule on the sphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latitude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: The graticule on the sphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The graticule is formed by the lines of constant latitude and constant longitude, which are constructed with reference to the rotation axis of the Earth. The primary reference points are the <a href="/wiki/Geographical_pole" title="Geographical pole">poles</a> where the axis of rotation of the Earth intersects the reference surface. Planes which contain the rotation axis intersect the surface at the <a href="/wiki/Meridian_(geography)" title="Meridian (geography)">meridians</a>; and the angle between any one meridian plane and that through Greenwich (the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Meridian" class="mw-redirect" title="Prime Meridian">Prime Meridian</a>) defines the longitude: meridians are lines of constant longitude. The plane through the centre of the Earth and perpendicular to the rotation axis intersects the surface at a great circle called the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">Equator</a>. Planes parallel to the equatorial plane intersect the surface in circles of constant latitude; these are the parallels. The Equator has a latitude of 0\xc2\xb0, the <a href="/wiki/North_Pole" title="North Pole">North Pole</a> has a latitude of 90\xc2\xb0 North (written 90\xc2\xb0&#160;N or +90\xc2\xb0), and the <a href="/wiki/South_Pole" title="South Pole">South Pole</a> has a latitude of 90\xc2\xb0 South (written 90\xc2\xb0&#160;S or \xe2\x88\x9290\xc2\xb0). The latitude of an arbitrary point is the angle between the equatorial plane and the normal to the surface at that point: the normal to the surface of the sphere is along the radius vector.</p>\n<p>The latitude, as defined in this way for the sphere, is often termed the spherical latitude, to avoid ambiguity with the geodetic latitude and the auxiliary latitudes defined in subsequent sections of this article.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Named_latitudes_on_the_Earth">Named latitudes on the Earth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latitude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Named latitudes on the Earth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:December_solstice_geometry.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/December_solstice_geometry.svg/300px-December_solstice_geometry.svg.png" width="300" height="205" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/December_solstice_geometry.svg/450px-December_solstice_geometry.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/December_solstice_geometry.svg/600px-December_solstice_geometry.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="367" data-file-height="251" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:December_solstice_geometry.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe orientation of the Earth at the December solstice.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Besides the equator, four other parallels are of significance:</p>\n<dl>\n<dd>\n<table class="wikitable" border="1">\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Arctic_Circle" title="Arctic Circle">Arctic Circle</a></td>\n<td>66\xc2\xb0 34\xe2\x80\xb2 (66.57\xc2\xb0) N</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Tropic_of_Cancer" title="Tropic of Cancer">Tropic of Cancer</a></td>\n<td>23\xc2\xb0 26\xe2\x80\xb2 (23.43\xc2\xb0) N</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Tropic_of_Capricorn" title="Tropic of Capricorn">Tropic of Capricorn</a></td>\n<td>23\xc2\xb0 26\xe2\x80\xb2 (23.43\xc2\xb0) S</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Circle" title="Antarctic Circle">Antarctic Circle</a></td>\n<td>66\xc2\xb0 34\xe2\x80\xb2 (66.57\xc2\xb0) S</td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n</dd>\n</dl>\n<p>The plane of the Earth\'s orbit about the Sun is called the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a>, and the plane perpendicular to the rotation axis of the Earth is the equatorial plane. The angle between the ecliptic and the equatorial plane is called variously the axial tilt, the obliquity, or the inclination of the ecliptic, and it is conventionally denoted by <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">i</span>. The latitude of the tropical circles is equal to <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">i</span> and the latitude of the polar circles is its complement (90\xc2\xb0 - <i>i</i>). The axis of rotation varies slowly over time and the values given here are those for the current <a href="/wiki/Epoch_(astronomy)" title="Epoch (astronomy)">epoch</a>. The time variation is discussed more fully in the article on <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">axial tilt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[b]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The figure shows the geometry of a <a href="/wiki/Cross_section_(geometry)" title="Cross section (geometry)">cross-section</a> of the plane perpendicular to the ecliptic and through the centres of the Earth and the Sun at the December <a href="/wiki/Solstice" title="Solstice">solstice</a> when the Sun is overhead at some point of the <a href="/wiki/Tropic_of_Capricorn" title="Tropic of Capricorn">Tropic of Capricorn</a>. The south polar latitudes below the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_Circle" title="Antarctic Circle">Antarctic Circle</a> are in daylight, whilst the north polar latitudes above the Arctic Circle are in night. The situation is reversed at the June solstice, when the Sun is overhead at the Tropic of Cancer. Only at latitudes in between the two <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a> is it possible for the Sun to be directly overhead (at the <a href="/wiki/Zenith" title="Zenith">zenith</a>).</p>\n<p>On <a href="/wiki/Map_projections" class="mw-redirect" title="Map projections">map projections</a> there is no universal rule as to how meridians and parallels should appear. The examples below show the named parallels (as red lines) on the commonly used <a href="/wiki/Mercator_projection" title="Mercator projection">Mercator projection</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Transverse_Mercator_projection" title="Transverse Mercator projection">Transverse Mercator projection</a>. On the former the parallels are horizontal and the meridians are vertical, whereas on the latter there is no exact relationship of parallels and meridians with horizontal and vertical: both are complicated curves.</p>\n<table style="margin: 1em auto 1em auto">\n<tr valign="top">\n<th width="1%"></th>\n<th width="36%">Normal Mercator</th>\n<th width="3%"></th>\n<th width="36%">Transverse Mercator</th>\n<th width="1%"></th>\n</tr>\n<tr valign="top">\n<td></td>\n<td align="center" width="200px">\n<div class="center">\n<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:MercNormSph_enhanced.png" alt="MercTranSph enhanced.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/MercTranSph_enhanced.png/200px-MercTranSph_enhanced.png" width="200" height="201" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/MercTranSph_enhanced.png/300px-MercTranSph_enhanced.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/MercTranSph_enhanced.png/400px-MercTranSph_enhanced.png 2x" data-file-width="1068" data-file-height="1073" /></a></div>\n</div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Meridian_distance_on_the_sphere">Meridian distance on the sphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latitude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Meridian distance on the sphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>On the sphere the normal passes through the centre and the latitude (<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xcf\x86</span>) is therefore equal to the angle subtended at the centre by the meridian arc from the equator to the point concerned. If the <a href="/wiki/Meridian_arc" title="Meridian arc">meridian distance</a> is denoted by <span class="texhtml"><i>m</i>(<i>\xcf\x86</i>)</span> then</p>\n<dl>\n<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >\n  <semantics>\n    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n        <mi>m</mi>\n        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n        <mi>&#x03D5;<!-- \xcf\x95 --></mi>\n        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n        <mo>=</mo>\n        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n          <mfrac>\n            <mi>&#x03C0;<!-- \xcf\x80 --></mi>\n            <msup>\n              <mn>180</mn>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                <mo>&#x2218;<!-- \xe2\x88\x98 --></mo>\n              </mrow>\n            </msup>\n          </mfrac>\n        </mrow>\n        <mi>R</mi>\n        <msub>\n          <mi>&#x03D5;<!-- \xcf\x95 --></mi>\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n              <mi mathvariant="normal">d</mi>\n              <mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi>\n              <mi mathvariant="normal">g</mi>\n              <mi mathvariant="normal">r</mi>\n              <mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi>\n              <mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi>\n              <mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi>\n            </mrow>\n          </mrow>\n        </msub>\n        <mo>=</mo>\n        <mi>R</mi>\n        <msub>\n          <mi>&#x03D5;<!-- \xcf\x95 --></mi>\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n              <mi mathvariant="normal">r</mi>\n              <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>\n              <mi mathvariant="normal">d</mi>\n              <mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi>\n              <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>\n              <mi mathvariant="normal">n</mi>\n              <mi mathvariant="normal">s</mi>\n            </mrow>\n          </mrow>\n        </msub>\n      </mstyle>\n    </mrow>\n    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle m(\\phi )={\\frac {\\pi }{180^{\\circ }}}R\\phi _{\\mathrm {degrees} }=R\\phi _{\\mathrm {radians} }}</annotation>\n  </semantics>\n</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/508adf4208d93637637efa0bca3d57f2a6613bc0" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:34.26ex; height:4.843ex;" alt="{\\displaystyle m(\\phi )={\\frac {\\pi }{180^{\\circ }}}R\\phi _{\\mathrm {degrees} }=R\\phi _{\\mathrm {radians} }}" /></span></dd>\n</dl>\n<p>where <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">R</span> denotes the <a href="/wiki/Earth_radius#Mean_radii" title="Earth radius">mean radius</a> of the Earth. <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">R</span> is equal to 6,371&#160;km or 3,959 miles. No higher accuracy is appropriate for <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">R</span> since higher-precision results necessitate an ellipsoid model. With this value for <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">R</span> the meridian length of 1 degree of latitude on the sphere is 111.2&#160;km or 69.1 miles. The length of 1 minute of latitude is 1.853&#160;km or 1.151 miles, and this distance was formerly used as the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Nautical_mile" title="Nautical mile">nautical mile</a>.</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Latitude_on_the_ellipsoid">Latitude on the ellipsoid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latitude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Latitude on the ellipsoid">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ellipsoids">Ellipsoids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latitude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ellipsoids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1687 <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a> published the <i><a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophi\xc3\xa6 Naturalis Principia Mathematica">Philosophi\xc3\xa6 Naturalis Principia Mathematica</a></i>, in which he proved that a rotating self-gravitating fluid body in equilibrium takes the form of an <a href="/wiki/Oblate" title="Oblate">oblate</a> ellipsoid.<sup id="cite_ref-newton_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newton-3">[1]</a></sup> (This article uses the term <i>ellipsoid</i> in preference to the older term <i>spheroid</i>.) Newton\'s result was confirmed by geodetic measurements in the 18th century. (See <a href="/wiki/Meridian_arc" title="Meridian arc">Meridian arc</a>.) An oblate ellipsoid is the three-dimensional surface generated by the rotation of an ellipse about its shorter axis (minor axis). "Oblate ellipsoid of revolution" is abbreviated to \'ellipsoid\' in the remainder of this article. (Ellipsoids which do not have an axis of symmetry are termed triaxial.)</p>\n<p>Many different <a href="/wiki/Figure_of_the_Earth" title="Figure of the Earth">reference ellipsoids</a> have been used in the history of <a href="/wiki/Geodesy" title="Geodesy">geodesy</a>. In pre-satellite days they were devised to give a good fit to the <a href="/wiki/Geoid" title="Geoid">geoid</a> over the limited area of a survey but, with the advent of <a href="/wiki/GPS" class="mw-redirect" title="GPS">GPS</a>, it has become natural to use reference ellipsoids (such as <a href="/wiki/WGS84" class="mw-redirect" title="WGS84">WGS84</a>) with centre at the centre of mass of the Earth and minor axis aligned to the rotation axis of the Earth. These geocentric ellipsoids are usually within 100&#160;m (330&#160;ft) of the geoid. Since latitude is defined with respect to an ellipsoid, the position of a given point is different on each ellipsoid: one cannot exactly specify the latitude and longitude of a geographical feature without specifying the ellipsoid used. Many maps maintained by national agencies are based on older ellipsoids, so one must know how the latitude and longitude values are transformed from one ellipsoid to another. GPS handsets include software to carry out <a href="/wiki/Datum_(geodesy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Datum (geodesy)">datum transformations</a> which link WGS84 to the local reference ellipsoid with its associated grid.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_geometry_of_the_ellipsoid">The geometry of the ellipsoid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latitude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: The geometry of the ellipsoid">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The shape of an ellipsoid of revolution is determined by the shape of the <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">ellipse</a> which is rotated about its minor (shorter) axis. Two parameters are required. One is invariably the equatorial radius, which is the <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">semi-major axis</a>, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">a</span>. The other parameter is usually (1)&#160;the polar radius or <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">semi-minor axis</a>, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">b</span>; or (2)&#160;the (first) <a href="/wiki/Flattening" title="Flattening">flattening</a>, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">f</span>; or (3)&#160;the <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">eccentricity</a>, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">e</span>. These parameters are not independent: they are related by</p>\n<dl>\n<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >\n  <semantics>\n    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n        <mi>f</mi>\n        <mo>=</mo>\n        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n          <mfrac>\n            <mrow>\n              <mi>a</mi>\n              <mo>&#x2212;<!-- \xe2\x88\x92 --></mo>\n              <mi>b</mi>\n            </mrow>\n            <mi>a</mi>\n          </mfrac>\n        </mrow>\n        <mo>,</mo>\n        <mspace width="2em" />\n        <msup>\n          <mi>e</mi>\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <mn>2</mn>\n          </mrow>\n        </msup>\n        <mo>=</mo>\n        <mn>2</mn>\n        <mi>f</mi>\n        <mo>&#x2212;<!-- \xe2\x88\x92 --></mo>\n        <msup>\n          <mi>f</mi>\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <mn>2</mn>\n          </mrow>\n        </msup>\n        <mo>,</mo>\n        <mspace width="2em" />\n        <mi>b</mi>\n        <mo>=</mo>\n        <mi>a</mi>\n        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n        <mn>1</mn>\n        <mo>&#x2212;<!-- \xe2\x88\x92 --></mo>\n        <mi>f</mi>\n        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n        <mo>=</mo>\n        <mi>a</mi>\n        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n          <msqrt>\n            <mn>1</mn>\n            <mo>&#x2212;<!-- \xe2\x88\x92 --></mo>\n            <msup>\n              <mi>e</mi>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                <mn>2</mn>\n              </mrow>\n            </msup>\n          </msqrt>\n        </mrow>\n        <mspace width="thinmathspace" />\n        <mo>.</mo>\n      </mstyle>\n    </mrow>\n    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle f={\\frac {a-b}{a}},\\qquad e^{2}=2f-f^{2},\\qquad b=a(1-f)=a{\\sqrt {1-e^{2}}}\\,.}</annotation>\n  </semantics>\n</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d176b5a394f9d591c6242a6e8754f0c3a6e76e90" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:61.089ex; height:5.343ex;" alt="{\\displaystyle f={\\frac {a-b}{a}},\\qquad e^{2}=2f-f^{2},\\qquad b=a(1-f)=a{\\sqrt {1-e^{2}}}\\,.}" /></span></dd>\n</dl>\n<p>Many other parameters (see <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">ellipse</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ellipsoid" title="Ellipsoid">ellipsoid</a>) appear in the study of geodesy, geophysics and map projections but they can all be expressed in terms of one or two members of the set <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">a</span>, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">b</span>, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">f</span> and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">e</span>. Both <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">f</span> and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">e</span> are small and often appear in series expansions in calculations; they are of the order <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">1</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">300</span></span> and 0.08 respectively. Values for a number of ellipsoids are given in <a href="/wiki/Figure_of_the_Earth" title="Figure of the Earth">Figure of the Earth</a>. Reference ellipsoids are usually defined by the semi-major axis and the <i>inverse</i> flattening, <span class="texhtml"><span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">1</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;"><i>f</i></span></span></span>. For example, the defining values for the <a href="/wiki/WGS84" class="mw-redirect" title="WGS84">WGS84</a> ellipsoid, used by all GPS devices, are<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[2]</a></sup></p>\n<ul>\n<li><span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">a</span> (equatorial radius): <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7006637813700000000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>6<span style="margin-left:.25em;">378</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">137</span>.0&#160;m</span> exactly</li>\n<li><span class="texhtml"><span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;">1</span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;"><i>f</i></span></span></span> (inverse flattening): <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7002298257223563000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>298.257<span style="margin-left:.25em;">223</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">563</span></span> exactly</li>\n</ul>\n<p>from which are derived</p>\n<ul>\n<li><span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">b</span> (polar radius): <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7006635675231420000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>6<span style="margin-left:.25em;">356</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">752</span>.3142&#160;m</span></li>\n<li><span class="texhtml"><i>e</i><sup>2</sup></span> (eccentricity squared): <span class="nowrap"><span style="display:none" class="sortkey">6997669437999014000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>0.006<span style="margin-left:.25em;">694</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">379</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">990</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">14</span></span></li>\n</ul>\n<p>The difference between the semi-major and semi-minor axes is about 21&#160;km (13 miles) and as fraction of the semi-major axis it equals the flattening; on a computer monitor the ellipsoid could be sized as 300 by 299 pixels. This would barely be distinguishable from a 300-by-300-pixel sphere, so illustrations usually exaggerate the flattening.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geodetic_and_geocentric_latitudes">Geodetic and geocentric latitudes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latitude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Geodetic and geocentric latitudes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Latitude_and_longitude_graticule_on_an_ellipsoid.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Latitude_and_longitude_graticule_on_an_ellipsoid.svg/200px-Latitude_and_longitude_graticule_on_an_ellipsoid.svg.png" width="200" height="182" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Latitude_and_longitude_graticule_on_an_ellipsoid.svg/300px-Latitude_and_longitude_graticule_on_an_ellipsoid.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Latitude_and_longitude_graticule_on_an_ellipsoid.svg/400px-Latitude_and_longitude_graticule_on_an_ellipsoid.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="825" data-file-height="750" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Latitude_and_longitude_graticule_on_an_ellipsoid.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe definition of geodetic latitude (<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xcf\x86</span>) and longitude (<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xce\xbb</span>) on an ellipsoid. The normal to the surface does not pass through the centre, except at the equator and at the poles.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The graticule on the ellipsoid is constructed in exactly the same way as on the sphere. The normal at a point on the surface of an ellipsoid does not pass through the centre, except for points on the equator or at the poles, but the definition of latitude remains unchanged as the angle between the normal and the equatorial plane. The terminology for latitude must be made more precise by distinguishing:</p>\n<ul>\n<li><b>Geodetic latitude:</b> the angle between the normal and the equatorial plane. The standard notation in English publications is <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xcf\x86</span>. This is the definition assumed when the word latitude is used without qualification. The definition must be accompanied with a specification of the ellipsoid.</li>\n<li><b>Geocentric latitude:</b> the angle between the radius (from centre to the point on the surface) and the equatorial plane. (Figure <a href="#Geocentric_latitude">below</a>). There is no standard notation: examples from various texts include <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xcf\x88</span>, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">q</span>, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xcf\x86\xe2\x80\xb2</span>, <span class="texhtml"><i>\xcf\x86</i><sub>c</sub></span>, <span class="texhtml"><i>\xcf\x86</i><sub>g</sub></span>. This article uses <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xcf\x88</span>.</li>\n<li><b>Spherical latitude:</b> the angle between the normal to a spherical reference surface and the equatorial plane.</li>\n<li><b>Geographic latitude</b> must be used with care. Some authors use it as a synonym for geodetic latitude whilst others use it as an alternative to the <a href="#Astronomical_latitude">astronomical latitude</a>.</li>\n<li><b>Latitude</b> (unqualified) should normally refer to the geodetic latitude.</li>\n</ul>\n<p>The importance of specifying the reference datum may be illustrated by a simple example. On the reference ellipsoid for WGS84, the centre of the <a href="/wiki/Eiffel_Tower" title="Eiffel Tower">Eiffel Tower</a> has a geodetic latitude of 48\xc2\xb0&#160;51\xe2\x80\xb2&#160;29\xe2\x80\xb3&#160;N, or 48.8583\xc2\xb0&#160;N and longitude of 2\xc2\xb0&#160;17\xe2\x80\xb2&#160;40\xe2\x80\xb3&#160;E or 2.2944\xc2\xb0E. The same coordinates on the datum <a href="/wiki/ED50" title="ED50">ED50</a> define a point on the ground which is 140 metres (460 feet) distant from the tower.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> A web search may produce several different values for the latitude of the tower; the reference ellipsoid is rarely specified.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Length_of_a_degree_of_latitude">Length of a degree of latitude</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a meridian distance integral is central to many studies in geodesy and map projection. It can be evaluated by expanding the integral by the binomial series and integrating term by term: see <a href="/wiki/Meridian_arc" title="Meridian arc">Meridian arc</a> for details. The length of the meridian arc between two given latitudes is given by replacing the limits of the integral by the latitudes concerned. href="/wiki/Length_of_a_degree_of_longitude" class="mw-redirect" title="Length of a degree of longitude">length of a degree of longitude</a> (east-west distance):</p>\n<dl>\n<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >\n  <semantics>\n    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n        <msubsup>\n          <mi mathvariant="normal">&#x0394;<!-- \xce\x94 --></mi>\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n              <mi mathvariant="normal">l</mi>\n              <mi mathvariant="normal">o</mi>\n              <mi mathvariant="normal">n</mi>\n              <mi mathvariant="normal">g</mi>\n            </mrow>\n          </mrow>\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <mn>1</mn>\n          </mrow>\n        </msubsup>\n        <mo>=</mo>\n        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n          <mfrac>\n            <mrow>\n              <mi>&#x03C0;<!-- \xcf\x80 --></mi>\n              <mi>a</mi>\n              <mi>cos</mi>\n              <mo>&#x2061;<!-- \xe2\x81\xa1 --></mo>\n              <mi>&#x03D5;<!-- \xcf\x95 --></mi>\n            </mrow>\n            <mrow>\n              <msup>\n                <mn>180</mn>\n                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                  <mo>&#x2218;<!-- \xe2\x88\x98 --></mo>\n                </mrow>\n              </msup>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                <msqrt>\n                  <mn>1</mn>\n                  <mo>&#x2212;<!-- \xe2\x88\x92 --></mo>\n                  <msup>\n {1-e^{2}\\sin ^{2}\\phi }}}}\\,}</annotation>\n  </semantics>\n</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6c56621cec1272fc6cc6bfd9e8f15e4e15adf620" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -4.671ex; width:28.768ex; height:8.343ex;" alt="{\\displaystyle \\Delta _{\\mathrm {long} }^{1}={\\frac {\\pi a\\cos \\phi }{180^{\\circ }{\\sqrt {1-e^{2}\\sin ^{2}\\phi }}}}\\,}" /></span></dd>\n</dl>\n<p>A calculator for any latitude is provided by the U.S. Government\'s <a href="/wiki/National_Geospatial-Intelligence_Agency" title="National Geospatial-Intelligence Agency">National Geospatial-Intelligence Agency</a> (NGA).<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[6]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Historically a <a href="/wiki/Nautical_mile" title="Nautical mile">nautical mile</a> was defined as the length of one minute of arc along a meridian of a spherical earth. An ellipsoid model leads to a variation of the nautical mile with latitude. This was resolved by defining the nautical mile to be exactly 1,852 metres.</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Auxiliary_latitudes">Auxiliary latitudes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latitude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Auxiliary latitudes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>There are six <b>auxiliary latitudes</b> that have applications to special problems in geodesy, geophysics and the theory of map projections:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>Geocentric latitude</li>\n<li>Reduced (or parametric) latitude</li>\n<li>Rectifying latitude</li>\n<li>Authalic latitude</li>\n<li>Conformal latitude</li>\n<li>Isometric latitude</li>\n</ul>\n<p>The definitions given in this section all relate to locations on the reference ellipsoid but the first two auxiliary latitudes, like the geodetic latitude, can be extended to define a three-dimensional <a href="/wiki/Geographic_coordinate_system" title="Geographic coordinate system">geographic coordinate system</a> as discussed <a href="#Latitude_and_coordinate_systems">below</a>. The remaining latitudes are not used in this way; they are used <i>only</i> as intermediate constructs in map projections of the reference ellipsoid to the plane or in calculations of geodesics on the ellipsoid. Their numerical values are not of interest. For example, no one would need to calculate the authalic latitude of the Eiffel Tower.</p>\n<p>The expressions below give the auxiliary latitudes in terms of the geodetic latitude, the semi-major axis, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">a</span>, and the eccentricity, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">e</span>. (For inverses see <a href="#Inverse_formulae_and_series">below</a>.) The forms given are, apart from notational variants, those in the standard reference for map projections, namely "Map projections: a working manual" by J. P. Snyder.<sup id="cite_ref-snyder_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-snyder-9">[7]</a></sup> Derivations of these expressions may be found in Adams<sup id="cite_ref-adams1921_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-adams1921-10">[8]</a></sup> and online publications by Osborne<sup id="cite_ref-osborne_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-osborne-6">[4]</a></sup> and Rapp.<sup id="cite_ref-rapp_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rapp-7">[5]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geocentric_latitude">Geocentric latitude</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latitude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Geocentric latitude">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Two-types-of-latitude.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Two-types-of-latitude.png/250px-Two-types-of-latitude.png" width="250" height="161" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Two-types-of-latitude.png/375px-Two-types-of-latitude.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Two-types-of-latitude.png/500px-Two-types-of-latitude.png 2x" data-file-width="787" data-file-height="508" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Two-types-of-latitude.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe definition of geodetic (or geographic) and geocentric latitudes.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The <b>geocentric latitude</b> is the angle between the equatorial plane and the radius from the centre to a point on the surface. The relation between the geocentric latitude (<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xcf\x88</span>) and the geodetic latitude (<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xcf\x86</span>) is derived in the above references as</p>\n<dl>\n<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >\n  <semantics>\n    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n <mi>tan</mi>\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <mo>&#x2212;<!-- \xe2\x88\x92 --></mo>\n            <mn>1</mn>\n          </mrow>\n        </msup>\n        <mo>&#x2061;<!-- \xe2\x81\xa1 --></mo>\n        <mrow>\n          <mo>(</mo>\n          <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n          <mn>1</mn>\n          <mo>&#x2212;<!-- \xe2\x88\x92 --></mo>\n          <msup>\n            <mi>e</mi>\n            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n              <mn>2</mn>\n            </mrow>\n          </msup>\n          <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n          <mi>tan</mi>\n          <mo>&#x2061;<!-- \xe2\x81\xa1 --></mo>\n          <mi>&#x03D5;<!-- \xcf\x95 --></mi>\n          <mo>)</mo>\n        </mrow>\n        <mo>=</mo>\n        <msup>\n          <mi>tan</mi>\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <mo>&#x2212;<!-- \xe2\x88\x92 --></mo>\n            <mn>1</mn>\n          </mrow>\n        </msup>\n        <mo>&#x2061;<!-- \xe2\x81\xa1 --></mo>\n        <mrow>\n          <mo>(</mo>\n          <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n          <mn>1</mn>\n          <mo>&#x2212;<!-- \xe2\x88\x92 --></mo>\n          <mi>f</mi>\n          <msup>\n            <mo <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\psi (\\phi )=\\tan ^{-1}\\left((1-e^{2})\\tan \\phi \\right)=\\tan ^{-1}\\left((1-f)^{2}\\tan \\phi \\right)\\,.}</annotation>\n  </semantics>\n</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/26937862df458ad1baaa0cc733ce5ae51cecdf23" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" \\right)\\,.}" /></span></dd>\n</dl>\n<p>The geodetic and geocentric latitudes are equal at the equator and at the poles but at other latitudes they differ by a few minutes of arc. Taking the value of the squared eccentricity as 0.0067 (it depends on the choice of ellipsoid) the maximum difference of <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >\n  <semantics>\n    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n        <mi>&#x03D5;<!-- \xcf\x95 --></mi>\n        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n          <mo>&#x2212;<!-- \xe2\x88\x92 --></mo>\n        </mrow>\n        <mi>&#x03C8;<!-- \xcf\x88 --></mi>\n      </mstyle>\n    </mrow>\n    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\phi {-}\\psi }</annotation>\n  </semantics>\n</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/559d8144cd7e4e4a1da7289de7aba7fa3573f210" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:4.738ex; height:2.509ex;" alt="{\\displaystyle \\phi {-}\\psi }" /></span> may be shown to be about 11.5 minutes of arc at a geodetic latitude of approximately 45\xc2\xb0&#160;6\xe2\x80\xb2.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[c]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span id="Reduced_(or_parametric)_latitude"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Reduced_.28or_parametric.29_latitude">Reduced (or parametric) latitude</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latitude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Reduced (or parametric) latitude">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellipsoid_reduced_angle_definition.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Ellipsoid_reduced_angle_definition.svg/200px-Ellipsoid_reduced_angle_definition.svg.png" width="200" height="195" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Ellipsoid_reduced_angle_definition.svg/300px-Ellipsoid_reduced_angle_definition.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Ellipsoid_reduced_angle_definition.svg/400px-Ellipsoid_reduced_angle_definition.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="330" data-file-height="321" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellipsoid_reduced_angle_definition.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nDefinition of the reduced latitude (<span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xce\xb2</span>) on the ellipsoid.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The <b>reduced</b> or <b>parametric latitude</b>, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xce\xb2</span>, is defined by the radius drawn from the centre of the ellipsoid to that point <span class="texhtml">Q</span> on the surrounding sphere (of radius <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">a</span>) which is the projection parallel to the Earth\'s axis of a point <span class="texhtml">P</span> on the ellipsoid at latitude <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xcf\x86</span>. It was introduced by Legendre<sup id="cite_ref-legendre_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-legendre-12">[9]</a></sup> and Bessel<sup id="cite_ref-bessel_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bessel-13">[10]</a></sup> who solved problems for geodesics on the ellipsoid by transforming them to an equivalent problem for spherical geodesics by using this smaller latitude. Bessel\'s notation, <span class="texhtml"><i>u</i>(<i>\xcf\x86</i>)</span>, is also used in the current literature. The reduced latitude is related to the geodetic latitude by:<sup id="cite_ref-osborne_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-osborne-6">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rapp_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rapp-7">[5]</a></sup></p>\n<dl>\n<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >\n  <semantics>\n    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n        <mi>&#x03B2;<!-- \xce\xb2 --></mi>\n        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n        <mi>&#x03D5;<!-- \xcf\x95 --></mi>\n        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n        <mo>=</mo>\n        <msup>\n          <mi>tan</mi>\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <mo>&#x2212;<!-- \xe2\x88\x92 --></mo>\n            <mn>1</mn>\n          </mrow>\n        </msup>\n        <mo>&#x2061;<!-- \xe2\x81\xa1 --></mo>\n        <mrow>\n          <mo>(</mo>\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <msqrt>\n              <mn>1</mn>\n              <mo>&#x2212;<!-- \xe2\x88\x92 --></mo>\n              <msup>\n                <mi>e</mi>\n                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                  <mn>2</mn>\n                </mrow>\n              </msup>\n            </msqrt>\n          </mrow>\n          <mi>tan</mi>\n          <mo>&#x2061;<!-- \xe2\x81\xa1 --></mo>\n          <mi>&#x03D5;<!-- \xcf\x95 --></mi>\n          <mo>)</mo>\n        </mrow>\n        <mo>=</mo>\n        <msup>\n          <mi>tan</mi>\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <mo>&#x2212;<!-- \xe2\x88\x92 --></mo>\n            <mn>1</mn>\n          </mrow>\n        </msup>\n        <mo>&#x2061;<!-- \xe2\x81\xa1 --></mo>\n        <mrow>\n          <mo>(</mo>\n          <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n          <mn>1</mn>\n          <mo>&#x2212;<!-- \xe2\x88\x92 --></mo>\n          <mi>f</mi>\n          <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n          <mi>tan</mi>\n          <mo>&#x2061;<!-- \xe2\x81\xa1 --></mo>\n          <mi>&#x03D5;<!-- \xcf\x95 --></mi>\n          <mo>)</mo>\n        </mrow>\n      </mstyle>\n    </mrow>\n    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\beta (\\phi )=\\tan ^{-1}\\left({\\sqrt {1-e^{2}}}\\tan \\phi \\right)=\\tan ^{-1}\\left((1-f)\\tan \\phi \\right)}</annotation>\n  </semantics>\n</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/73a42a66b39844ce024d63ac0ffeb3c4ac3b08d4" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:53.669ex; height:4.843ex;" alt="{\\displaystyle \\beta (\\phi )=\\tan ^{-1}\\left({\\sqrt {1-e^{2}}}\\tan \\phi \\right)=\\tan ^{-1}\\left((1-f)\\tan \\phi \\right)}" /></span></dd>\n</dl>\n<p>The alternative name arises from the parameterization of the equation of the ellipse describing a meridian section. In terms of Cartesian coordinates <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">p</span>, the distance from the minor axis, and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">z</span>, the distance above the equatorial plane, the equation of the <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">ellipse</a> is:</p>\n<dl>\n<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >\n  <semantics>\n    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n          <mfrac>\n            <msup>\n              <mi>p</mi>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                <mn>2</mn>\n              </mrow>\n            </msup>\n            <msup>\n              <mi>a</mi>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                <mn>2</mn>\n              </mrow>\n            </msup>\n          </mfrac>\n        </mrow>\n        <mo>+</mo>\n        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n          <mfrac>\n            <msup>\n              <mi>z</mi>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                <mn>2</mn>\n              </mrow>\n            </msup>\n            <msup>\n              <mi>b</mi>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                <mn>2</mn>\n              </mrow>\n            </msup>\n          </mfrac>\n        </mrow>\n        <mo>=</mo>\n        <mn>1</mn>\n        <mspace width="thinmathspace" />\n        <mo>.</mo>\n      </mstyle>\n    </mrow>\n    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\frac {p^{2}}{a^{2}}}+{\\frac {z^{2}}{b^{2}}}=1\\,.}</annotation>\n  </semantics>\n</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9b55d30cf33f6eccc1558881edcfd2dfe423c5dd" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:14.314ex; height:6.009ex;" alt="{\\displaystyle {\\frac {p^{2}}{a^{2}}}+{\\frac {z^{2}}{b^{2}}}=1\\,.}" /></span></dd>\n</dl>\n<p>The Cartesian coordinates of the point are parameterized by</p>\n<dl>\n<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >\n  <semantics>\n    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n        <mi>p</mi>\n        <mo>=</mo>\n        <mi>a</mi>\n        <mi>cos</mi>\n        <mo>&#x2061;<!-- \xe2\x81\xa1 --></mo>\n        <mi>&#x03B2;<!-- \xce\xb2 --></mi>\n        <mspace width="thinmathspace" />\n        <mo>,</mo>\n        <mspace width="2em" />\n        <mi>z</mi>\n        <mo>=</mo>\n        <mi>b</mi>\n        <mi>sin</mi>\n        <mo>&#x2061;<!-- \xe2\x81\xa1 --></mo>\n        <mi>&#x03B2;<!-- \xce\xb2 --></mi>\n        <mspace width="thinmathspace" />\n        <mo>;</mo>\n      </mstyle>\n    </mrow>\n    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle p=a\\cos \\beta \\,,\\qquad z=b\\sin \\beta \\,;}</annotation>\n  </semantics>\n</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ab1ea1af54648e83df7ac4fba6091bbc9bc65b34" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; margin-left: -0.079ex; width:28.207ex; height:2.509ex;" alt="{\\displaystyle p=a\\cos \\beta \\,,\\qquad z=b\\sin \\beta \\,;}" /></span></dd>\n</dl>\n<p>Cayley suggested the term <i>parametric latitude</i> because of the form of these equations.<sup id="cite_ref-cayley_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cayley-14">[11]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The reduced latitude is not used in the theory of map projections. Its most important application is in the theory of ellipsoid geodesics. (<a href="/wiki/Vincenty%27s_formulae" title="Vincenty\'s formulae">Vincenty</a>, Karney).<sup id="cite_ref-Karney_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karney-15">[12]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rectifying_latitude">Rectifying latitude</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latitude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Rectifying latitude">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Rectifying_radius" class="mw-redirect" title="Rectifying radius">Rectifying radius</a></div>\n<p>The <b>rectifying latitude</b>, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xce\xbc</span>, is the meridian distance scaled so that its value at the poles is equal to 90 degrees or <span class="sfrac nowrap" style="display:inline-block; vertical-align:-0.5em; font-size:85%; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em;"><span class="texhtml">\xcf\x80</span></span><span class="visualhide">/</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em; margin:0 0.1em; border-top:1px solid;">2</span></span> radians:</p>\n<dl>\n<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >\n  <semantics>\n    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n      <mstyle }{2}}\\right)\\,.}" /></span></dd>\n</dl>\n<p>Using the rectifying latitude to define a latitude on a sphere of radius</p>\n<dl>\n<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >\n  <semantics>\n    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n        <mi>R</mi>\n        <mo>=</mo>\n        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n          <mfrac>\n            <mrow>\n              <mn>2</mn>\n              <msub>\n                <mi>m</mi>\n                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                    <mi mathvariant="normal">p</mi>\n                  </mrow>\n                </mrow>\n              </msub>\n            </mrow>\n            <mi>&#x03C0;<!-- \xcf\x80 --></mi>\n          </mfrac>\n        </mrow>\n      </mstyle>\n    </mrow>\n    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle R={\\frac {2m_{\\mathrm {p} }}{\\pi }}}</annotation>\n  </semantics>\n</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f3c5625835b98da55e6ea02beb168219a8abef06" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:10.097ex; height:5.509ex;" alt="{\\displaystyle R={\\frac {2m_{\\mathrm {p} }}{\\pi }}}" /></span></dd>\n</dl>\n<p>defines a projection from the ellipsoid to the sphere such that all meridians have true length and uniform scale. The sphere may then be projected to the plane with an <a href="/wiki/Equirectangular_projection" title="Equirectangular projection">equirectangular projection</a> to give a double projection from the ellipsoid to the plane such that all meridians have true length and uniform meridian scale. An example of the use of the rectifying latitude is the <a href="/wiki/Map_projection" title="Map projection">Equidistant conic projection</a>. (Snyder, Section 16).<sup id="cite_ref-snyder_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-snyder-9">[7]</a></sup> The rectifying latitude is also of great importance in the construction of the <a href="/wiki/Transverse_Mercator_projection" title="Transverse Mercator projection">Transverse Mercator projection</a>.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Authalic_latitude">Authalic {\\pi }{2}}\\right)&amp;=1-{\\frac {1-e^{2}}{2e}}\\ln \\left({\\frac {1-e}{1+e}}\\right)&amp;=1+{\\frac {1-e^{2}}{e}}\\tanh ^{-1}e\\end{aligned}}}" /></span></dd>\n</dl>\n<p>and the radius of the class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:\xc2\xa714</sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conformal_latitude">Conformal latitude</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span ^{-1}\\left[\\left({\\frac {1+\\sin \\phi }{1-\\sin \\phi }}\\right)\\left({\\frac {1-e\\sin \\phi }{1+e\\sin \\phi }}\\right)^{e}\\right]^{\\frac {1}{2}}-{\\frac {\\pi }{2}}\\\\[2ex]&amp;=2\\tan ^{-1}\\left[\\tan \\left({\\frac {\\phi }{2}}+{\\frac {\\pi }{4}}\\right)\\left({\\frac {1-e\\sin \\phi }{1+e\\sin \\phi }}\\right)^{\\frac {e}{2}}\\right]-{\\frac {\\pi }{2}}\\\\&amp;=\\sin ^{-1}\\left[\\tanh \\left(\\tanh ^{-1}(\\sin \\phi )-e\\tanh ^{-1}(e\\sin \\phi )\\right)\\right]\\\\&amp;=\\operatorname {gd} \\left[\\operatorname {gd} ^{-1}(\\phi )-e\\tanh ^{-1}(e\\sin \\phi )\\right]\\end{aligned}}}</annotation>\n  </semantics>\n</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/78a34a71d3042786a897449f83bab0ee59c5bc3c" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -11.435ex; margin-bottom: -0.236ex; width:55.488ex; height:24.509ex;" alt="{\\displaystyle {\\begin{aligned}\\chi (\\phi )&amp;=2\\tan ^{-1}\\left[\\left({\\frac {1+\\sin \\phi }{1-\\sin \\phi }}\\right)\\left({\\frac {1-e\\sin \\phi }{1+e\\sin \\phi }}\\right)^{e}\\right]^{\\frac {1}{2}}-{\\frac {\\pi }{2}}\\\\[2ex]&amp;=2\\tan ^{-1}\\left[\\tan \\left({\\frac {\\phi }{2}}+{\\frac {\\pi }{4}}\\right)\\left({\\frac {1-e\\sin \\phi }{1+e\\sin \\phi }}\\right)^{\\frac {e}{2}}\\right]-{\\frac {\\pi }{2}}\\\\&amp;=\\sin ^{-1}\\left[\\tanh \\left(\\tanh ^{-1}(\\sin \\phi )-e\\tanh ^{-1}(e\\sin \\phi )\\right)\\right]\\\\&amp;=\\operatorname {gd} \\left[\\operatorname {gd} ^{-1}(\\phi )-e\\tanh ^{-1}(e\\sin \\phi )\\right]\\end{aligned}}}" /></span></dd>\n</dl>\n<p>where <span class="texhtml">gd(<i>x</i>)</span> is the <a href="/wiki/Gudermannian_function" title="Gudermannian function">Gudermannian function</a>. (See also <a href="/wiki/Mercator_projection#Alternative_expressions" title="Mercator projection">Mercator projection</a>.) The conformal latitude defines a transformation from the ellipsoid to a sphere of <i>arbitrary</i> radius such that the angle of intersection between any two lines on the ellipsoid is the same as the corresponding angle on the sphere (so that the shape of <i>small</i> elements is well preserved). A further conformal transformation from the sphere to the plane gives a conformal double projection from the ellipsoid to the plane. This is not the only way of generating such a conformal projection. For example, the \'exact\' version of the <a href="/wiki/Transverse_Mercator_projection" title="Transverse Mercator projection">Transverse Mercator projection</a> on the ellipsoid is not a double projection. (It does, however, involve a generalisation of the conformal latitude to the complex plane).</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isometric_latitude">Isometric latitude</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latitude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Isometric latitude">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <b>isometric latitude</b> is conventionally denoted by <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xcf\x88</span> (not to be confused with the geocentric latitude): it is used in the development of the ellipsoidal versions of the normal <a href="/wiki/Mercator_projection" title="Mercator projection">Mercator projection</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Transverse_Mercator_projection" title="Transverse Mercator projection">Transverse Mercator projection</a>. The name "isometric" arises from the fact that at any point on the ellipsoid equal increments of <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xcf\x88</span> and longitude <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xce\xbb</span> give rise to equal distance displacements along the meridians and parallels respectively. The <a href="/wiki/Geographic_coordinate_system" title="Geographic coordinate system">graticule</a> defined by the lines of constant <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xcf\x88</span> and constant <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xce\xbb</span>, divides the surface of the ellipsoid into a mesh of squares (of varying size). The isometric latitude is zero at the equator but rapidly diverges from the geodetic latitude, tending to infinity at the poles. The conventional notation is given in Snyder (page 15):<sup id="cite_ref-snyder_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-snyder-9">[7]</a></sup></p>\n<dl>\n<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span ).\\end{aligned}}}</annotation>\n  </semantics>\n</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4c1a509c8c678529e02cdc46e4518cd39a04100a" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -5.838ex; width:47.835ex; height:12.843ex;" alt="{\\displaystyle {\\begin{aligned}\\psi (\\phi )&amp;=\\ln \\left[\\tan \\left({\\frac {\\pi }{4}}+{\\frac {\\phi }{2}}\\right)\\right]+{\\frac {e}{2}}\\ln \\left[{\\frac {1-e\\sin \\phi }{1+e\\sin \\phi }}\\right]\\\\&amp;=\\tanh ^{-1}(\\sin \\phi )-e\\tanh ^{-1}(e\\sin \\phi )\\\\&amp;=\\operatorname {gd} ^{-1}(\\phi )-e\\tanh ^{-1}(e\\sin \\phi ).\\end{aligned}}}" /></span></dd>\n</dl>\n<p>For the <i>normal</i> Mercator projection (on the ellipsoid) this function defines the spacing of the parallels: if the length of the equator on the projection is <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">E</span> (units of length or pixels) then the distance, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">y</span>, of a parallel of latitude <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xcf\x86</span> from the equator is</p>\n<dl>\n<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >\n  <semantics>\n    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n        <mi>y</mi>\n        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n        <mi>&#x03D5;<!-- \xcf\x95 --></mi>\n        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n        <mo>=</mo>\n        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n          <mfrac>\n            <mi>E</mi>\n            <mrow>\n              <mn>2</mn>\n              <mi>&#x03C0;<!-- \xcf\x80 --></mi>\n            </mrow>\n          </mfrac>\n        </mrow>\n        <mi>&#x03C8;<!-- \xcf\x88 --></mi>\n        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n        <mi>&#x03D5;<!-- \xcf\x95 --></mi>\n        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n        <mspace width="thinmathspace" />\n        <mo>.</mo>\n      </mstyle>\n    </mrow>\n    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle y(\\phi )={\\frac {E}{2\\pi }}\\psi (\\phi )\\,.}</annotation>\n  </semantics>\n</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4c3c8fdb803b473efe5685cc97cdcfe6622aa472" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:16.647ex; height:5.343ex;" alt="{\\displaystyle y(\\phi )={\\frac {E}{2\\pi }}\\psi (\\phi )\\,.}" /></span></dd>\n</dl>\n<p>The isometric latitude <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xcf\x88</span> is closely related to the conformal latitude <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xcf\x87</span>:</p>\n<dl>\n<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >\n  <semantics>\n    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n        <mi>&#x03C8;<!-- \xcf\x88 --></mi>\n        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n        <mi>&#x03D5;<!-- \xcf\x95 --></mi>\n        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n        <mo>=</mo>\n        <msup>\n          <mi>gd</mi>\n          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n            <mo>&#x2212;<!-- \xe2\x88\x92 --></mo>\n            <mn>1</mn>\n          </mrow>\n        </msup>\n        <mo>&#x2061;<!-- \xe2\x81\xa1 --></mo>\n        <mi>&#x03C7;<!-- \xcf\x87 --></mi>\n        <mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n        <mi>&#x03D5;<!-- \xcf\x95 --></mi>\n        <mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n        <mspace width="thinmathspace" />\n        <mo>.</mo>\n      </mstyle>\n    </mrow>\n    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\psi (\\phi )=\\operatorname {gd} ^{-1}\\chi (\\phi )\\,.}</annotation>\n  </semantics>\n</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7eaf60be567d5882f6b8a5b07f11fc3aafa2c157" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:18.805ex; height:3.176ex;" alt="{\\displaystyle \\psi (\\phi )=\\operatorname {gd} ^{-1}\\chi (\\phi )\\,.}" /></span></dd>\n</dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inverse_formulae_and_series">Inverse formulae and series</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latitude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Inverse formulae and series">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The formulae in the previous sections give the auxiliary latitude in terms of the geodetic latitude. The expressions for the geocentric and reduced latitudes may be inverted directly but this is impossible in the four remaining cases: the rectifying, authalic, conformal, and isometric latitudes. There are two methods of proceeding. The first is a numerical inversion of the defining equation for each and every particular value of the auxiliary latitude. The methods available are <a href="/wiki/Fixed-point_iteration" title="Fixed-point iteration">fixed-point iteration</a> and <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_method" title="Newton\'s method">Newton\xe2\x80\x93Raphson</a> root finding. The other, more useful, approach is to express the auxiliary latitude as a series in terms of the geodetic latitude and then invert the series by the method of <a href="/w/index.php?title=Lagrange_reversion&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Lagrange reversion (page does not exist)">Lagrange reversion</a>. Such series are presented by Adams who uses Taylor series expansions and gives coefficients in terms of the eccentricity.<sup id="cite_ref-adams1921_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-adams1921-10">[8]</a></sup> Osborne<sup id="cite_ref-osborne_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-osborne-6">[4]</a></sup> derives series to arbitrary order by using the computer algebra package Maxima<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[13]</a></sup> and expresses the coefficients in terms of both eccentricity and flattening. The series method is not applicable to the isometric latitude and one must use the conformal latitude in an intermediate step.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Numerical_comparison_of_auxiliary_latitudes">Numerical comparison of auxiliary latitudes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latitude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Numerical comparison of auxiliary latitudes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The following plot shows the magnitude of the difference between the geodetic latitude, (denoted as the "common" latitude on the plot), and the auxiliary latitudes other than the isometric latitude (which diverges to infinity at the poles). In every case the geodetic latitude is the greater. The differences shown on the plot are in arc minutes. The horizontal resolution of the plot fails to make clear that the maxima of the curves are not at 45\xc2\xb0 but calculation shows that they are within a few arc minutes of 45\xc2\xb0. Some representative data points are given in the table following the plot. Note the closeness of the conformal and geocentric latitudes. This was exploited in the days of hand calculators to expedite the construction of map projections.<sup id="cite_ref-snyder_9-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-snyder-9">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:108</sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Types_of_latitude_difference.png" class="image"><img alt="Types of latitude difference.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Types_of_latitude_difference.png/400px-Types_of_latitude_difference.png" width="400" height="300" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/dc/Types_of_latitude_difference.png 1.5x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="384" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Types_of_latitude_difference.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<table class="wikitable"
