style="text-align:right;">\n<td>60\xc2\xb0</td>\n<td>5.06\xe2\x80\xb2</td>\n<td>6.75\xe2\x80\xb2</td>\n<td>7.59\xe2\x80\xb2</td>\n<td>10.12\xe2\x80\xb2</td>\n<td>10.13\xe2\x80\xb2</td>\n</tr>\n<tr style="text-align:right;">\n<td>75\xc2\xb0</td>\n<td>2.92\xe2\x80\xb2</td>\n<td>3.90\xe2\x80\xb2</td>\n<td>4.39\xe2\x80\xb2</td>\n<td>5.85\xe2\x80\xb2</td>\n<td>5.85\xe2\x80\xb2</td>\n</tr>\n<tr style="text-align:right;">\n<td>90\xc2\xb0</td>\n<td>0.00\xe2\x80\xb2</td>\n<td>0.00\xe2\x80\xb2</td>\n<td>0.00\xe2\x80\xb2</td>\n<td>0.00\xe2\x80\xb2</td>\n<td>0.00\xe2\x80\xb2</td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Latitude_and_coordinate_systems">Latitude and coordinate systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latitude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Latitude and coordinate systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The geodetic latitude, or any of the auxiliary latitudes defined on the reference ellipsoid, constitutes with longitude a two-dimensional coordinate system on that ellipsoid. To define the position of an arbitrary point it is necessary to extend such a coordinate system into three dimensions. Three latitudes are used in this way: the geodetic, geocentric and reduced latitudes are used in geodetic coordinates, spherical polar coordinates and ellipsoidal coordinates respectively.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geodetic_coordinates">Geodetic coordinates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latitude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Geodetic coordinates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Geodetic_coordinates.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Geodetic_coordinates.svg/200px-Geodetic_coordinates.svg.png" width="200" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Geodetic_coordinates.svg/300px-Geodetic_coordinates.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Geodetic_coordinates.svg/400px-Geodetic_coordinates.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="268" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Geodetic_coordinates.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nGeodetic coordinates <span class="texhtml">P(<i>\xc9\xb8</i>,<i>\xce\xbb</i>,<i>h</i>)</span></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>At an arbitrary point <span class="texhtml">P</span> consider the line <span class="texhtml">PN</span> which is normal to the reference ellipsoid. The geodetic coordinates <span class="texhtml">P(<i>\xc9\xb8</i>,<i>\xce\xbb</i>,<i>h</i>)</span> are the latitude and longitude of the point <span class="texhtml">N</span> on the ellipsoid and the distance <span class="texhtml">PN</span>. This height differs from the height above the geoid or a reference height such as that above mean sea level at a specified location. The direction of <span class="texhtml">PN</span> will also differ from the direction of a vertical plumb line. The relation of these different heights requires knowledge of the shape of the geoid and also the gravity field of the Earth.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spherical_polar_coordinates">Spherical polar coordinates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latitude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Spherical polar coordinates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Geocentric_coordinates.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Geocentric_coordinates.svg/200px-Geocentric_coordinates.svg.png" width="200" height="191" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Geocentric_coordinates.svg/300px-Geocentric_coordinates.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Geocentric_coordinates.svg/400px-Geocentric_coordinates.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="286" data-file-height="273" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Geocentric_coordinates.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nGeocentric coordinate related to spherical polar coordinates <span class="texhtml">P(<i>r</i>,<i>\xce\xb8</i>,<i>\xce\xbb</i>)</span></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The geocentric latitude <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xcf\x88</span> is the complement of the polar angle <span class="texhtml">\xce\xb8</span> in conventional <a href="/wiki/Spherical_polar_coordinates" class="mw-redirect" title="Spherical polar coordinates">spherical polar coordinates</a> in which the coordinates of a point are <span class="texhtml">P(<i>r</i>,<i>\xce\xb8</i>,<i>\xce\xbb</i>)</span> where <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">r</span> is the distance of <span class="texhtml">P</span> from the centre <span class="texhtml">O</span>, <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xce\xb8</span> is the angle between the radius vector and the polar axis and <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xce\xbb</span> is longitude. Since the normal at a general point on the ellipsoid does not pass through the centre it is clear that points on the normal, which all have the same geodetic latitude, will have differing geocentric latitudes. Spherical polar coordinate systems are used in the analysis of the gravity field.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ellipsoidal_coordinates">Ellipsoidal coordinates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latitude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Ellipsoidal coordinates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellipsoidal_coordinates.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Ellipsoidal_coordinates.svg/200px-Ellipsoidal_coordinates.svg.png" width="200" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Ellipsoidal_coordinates.svg/300px-Ellipsoidal_coordinates.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Ellipsoidal_coordinates.svg/400px-Ellipsoidal_coordinates.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="299" data-file-height="288" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellipsoidal_coordinates.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nEllipsoidal coordinates <span class="texhtml">P(<i>u</i>,<i>\xce\xb2</i>,<i>\xce\xbb</i>)</span></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The reduced latitude can also be extended to a three-dimensional coordinate system. For a point <span class="texhtml">P</span> not on the reference ellipsoid (semi-axes <span class="texhtml">OA</span> and <span class="texhtml">OB</span>) construct an auxiliary ellipsoid which is confocal (same foci <span class="texhtml">F</span>, <span class="texhtml">F\xe2\x80\xb2</span>) with the reference ellipsoid: the necessary condition is that the product <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">ae</span> of semi-major axis and eccentricity is the same for both ellipsoids. Let <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">u</span> be the semi-minor axis (<span class="texhtml">OD</span>) of the auxiliary ellipsoid. Further let <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">\xce\xb2</span> be the reduced latitude of <span class="texhtml">P</span> on the auxiliary ellipsoid. The set <span class="texhtml">(<i>u</i>,<i>\xce\xb2</i>,<i>\xce\xbb</i>)</span> define the ellipsoid coordinates.<sup id="cite_ref-torge_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-torge-5">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:\xc2\xa74.2.2</sup> These coordinates are the natural choice in models of the gravity field for a uniform distribution of mass bounded by the reference ellipsoid.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coordinate_conversions">Coordinate conversions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latitude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Coordinate conversions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The relations between the above coordinate systems, and also Cartesian coordinates are not presented here. The transformation between geodetic and Cartesian coordinates may be found in <a href="/wiki/Geographic_coordinate_conversion" title="Geographic coordinate conversion">Geographic coordinate conversion</a>. The relation of Cartesian and spherical polars is given in <a href="/wiki/Spherical_coordinate_system" title="Spherical coordinate system">Spherical coordinate system</a>. The relation of Cartesian and ellipsoidal coordinates is discussed in Torge.<sup id="cite_ref-torge_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-torge-5">[3]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomical_latitude">Astronomical latitude</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latitude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Astronomical latitude">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p><b>Astronomical latitude</b> (<span class="texhtml">\xce\xa6</span>) is the angle between the equatorial plane and the true <a href="/wiki/Vertical_line" class="mw-redirect" title="Vertical line">vertical</a> at a point on the surface. The true vertical, the direction of a <a href="/wiki/Plumb_line" class="mw-redirect" title="Plumb line">plumb line</a>, is also the direction of the gravity acceleration, the resultant of the <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_acceleration" title="Gravitational acceleration">gravitational acceleration</a> (mass-based) and the <a href="/wiki/Centrifugal_acceleration" class="mw-redirect" title="Centrifugal acceleration">centrifugal acceleration</a> at that latitude.<sup id="cite_ref-torge_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-torge-5">[3]</a></sup> Astronomic latitude is calculated from angles measured between the <a href="/wiki/Zenith" title="Zenith">zenith</a> and stars whose <a href="/wiki/Declination" title="Declination">declination</a> is accurately known.</p>\n<p>In general the true vertical at a point on the surface does not exactly coincide with either the normal to the reference ellipsoid or the normal to the geoid. The angle between the astronomic and geodetic normals is usually a few seconds of arc but it is important in geodesy.<sup id="cite_ref-torge_5-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-torge-5">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wellenhofmoritz_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wellenhofmoritz-17">[14]</a></sup> The reason why it differs from the normal to the geoid is, because the geoid is an idealized, theoretical shape "at mean sea level". Points on the real surface of the earth are usually above or below this idealized geoid surface and here the true vertical can vary slightly. Also, the true vertical at a point at a specific time is influenced by tidal forces, which the theoretical geoid averages out.</p>\n<p>Astronomical latitude is not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Declination" title="Declination">declination</a>, the coordinate <a href="/wiki/Astronomer" title="Astronomer">astronomers</a> use in a similar way to specify the angular position of stars north/south of the <a href="/wiki/Celestial_equator" title="Celestial equator">celestial equator</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Equatorial_coordinates" class="mw-redirect" title="Equatorial coordinates">equatorial coordinates</a>), nor with <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic_latitude" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecliptic latitude">ecliptic latitude</a>, the coordinate that astronomers use to specify the angular position of stars north/south of the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic_coordinates" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecliptic coordinates">ecliptic coordinates</a>).</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latitude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 20em; -webkit-column-width: 20em; column-width: 20em;">\n<ul>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Altitude" title="Altitude">Altitude</a> (<a href="/wiki/Sea_level" title="Sea level">mean sea level</a>)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Bowditch%27s_American_Practical_Navigator" title="Bowditch\'s American Practical Navigator">Bowditch\'s American Practical Navigator</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Cardinal_direction" title="Cardinal direction">Cardinal direction</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Declination" title="Declination">Declination</a> on <a href="/w/index.php?title=Latitude&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The current full documentation of ISO 19111 may be purchased from <a rel="nofollow" class="external free" href="http://www.iso.org">http://www.iso.org</a> but drafts of the final standard are freely available at many web sites, one such is available at the following <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.seegrid.csiro.au/wiki/pub/Xmml/CoordinateReferenceSystems/19111_FDIS20021107.pdf">CSIRO</a></span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The value of this angle today is 23\xc2\xb026\xe2\x80\xb213.1\xe2\x80\xb3&#160;(or&#160;23.43697\xc2\xb0).
